RATING: 10
One of the most favorite wargame on board.

All of the Armies in starter packs is pretty balance itself. Each faction has unique advantages and abilities on its own makes the game memorizable. The game mechanics somehow a little complex but can get used to it on few mactches. The time required for a game is quite low (avg 30 mins). The mechanics of the game itself highly depends on unit placement and moderate of luck on unit/action drawing. This make each game not only has high replay value, but also create more possibility of 'playing on same faction match up. 

Strangely, this game resembles some elements of chess, which I enjoyed it.

On the other sides, each factions has limited playstyle due to specialed traits. Also, the game and its expansions do costs a fortune, espectially in Asia, so I rate this game 9/10. (However, I think this game deserve higher ranking so I rated it 10 in BGG)

RATING: 5
I'm not a big fan of luck-driven boardgames, but I actually really enjoy the game as an iPhone app - as such it works well as combat resolution is automatic and there's no downtime due to AP.. it's very fast and in a right kind of way, frustrating. I'd rate the [i]iPhone app[/i] around [size=12][BGCOLOR=#66FF99][COLOR=#66FF99]_[/COLOR][size=8][b]8[/b][/size][COLOR=#66FF99]_[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR][/size] - it's a highly addictive game I "love to hate". There's a lot of interesting tactical depth to this puzzle game despite the considerable luck element. 

However, as a [i]board game[/i] Neuroshima Hex is quite terrible.. Long downtime, AP, difficult calculations.. the game takes way too long for what it is. If the game would be short you wouldn't perhaps feel [i]quite[/i] as bad for being screwed by bad luck in the random tile draw, but the game always seems to take forever and people get frustrated and mad at each other. As a board game I'd rate it [size=12][BGCOLOR=#CC99FF][COLOR=#CC99FF]_[/COLOR][size=8][b]5[/b][/size][COLOR=#CC99FF]_[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR][/size].

One more thing that has to be said is that you really need to play the game dozens of times before you get a hang of it. Basically you have to familiarize yourself with every single tile in the game and memorize the content of every faction deck to be able to appreciate the tactical (can't really say "strategic" as the game is too random for that) side of it. Between highly experienced players the game is probably very interesting, but new players: expect your first 20 or so games to be chaotic and disappointing.

RATING: 8.5
Neuroshima Hex has this cool post-apocalyptic theme.  But it's the gameplay that's really engaging.  It's cool to look and see who will kill who when the time comes to fight, because that will often change over the course of the interim turns as some units move away, others come in to block ranged attacks, and still others come in that will kill the attacking unit before it gets a chance to attack!

The booster tiles and bases have interesting, thematic effects as well and definitely lend themselves to different types of play.  The different factions play differently against each other, and since your options are limited by the tiles you draw (and you can only use about 2/3 of your deck since you discard the other third) the games are very tactical and don't seem samey.

What happens away from the HQ doesn't seem to matter too much.

RATING: 6
Maybe I missed something.  I marvel at the comments this receives from my Geekbuddies.  It's not bad, but it's not all that and a bag of chips.

The name is Ameritrash, but the gameplay is Euro/abstract (think: [thing=9674][/thing]).  The only random part is the piece draw, which is pretty much what a worker-placement game has and this reminds me of.  You place them, you calculate your success.  Even the end game screams "Euro" (something similar to [thing=9209][/thing]), rewarding the guy who jumps out to an early lead and then churns through his pieces.  If your combat markers show up at the end, it's really hard to score hits, ya know, and if your final draw is 2 combat pieces -- well, what good is that?

I played 2 games, using the 4 factions in the box, and it seems to depend a lot on bad draws.  For instance, in one game, I drew 3 of my 4 combat markers in the first 10 pieces.  I had 2 out of my first 11 pieces that were actual combat units.  In another game, after a big combat, my opponent drew a lot of combat units and I could not.  It's not management as much as dumb luck.  I could calculate combat results and end game winners long before they happened -- again, that "Euro feel".

Maybe I need to play it more often: I'm open to it.  Maybe it needs a house rule, such as a limit of 4-5 tiles, instead of 3, and a requirement that you play no more than 2 and discard at least 1.  Or dividing the stacks up in units and tactics and having you select where you draw from.  But doing that just makes it more Euro, but in it's current state, it just seems like the game is misfiring on a cylinder.

RATING: 9
Neuroshima Hex! is a strategy game set in the post-apocalyptic world of Neuroshima, a Polish role-playing game. Each player leads one of four armies: Borgo, Hegemonia (Hegemony), Moloch, and Posterunek (Outpost). Each army deck consists of 34 tiles: soldiers, support tiles, and special actions. You win when all enemy headquarters are destroyed or when your headquarters is the least damaged at the end of the game.

RATING: 6
This was described to me as "A knife fight in a phonebooth."  Entirely accurate.  The gameplay is strategic and entertaining, with interesting choices and options.  However, it's also somewhat luck-based.  If you turn over great pieces, you'll likely do well.  While the other half is strategy, since the fighting is within the confines of a phonebooth-sized piece of real estate, it's quick and dirty.  Strategy plays a part, but it's not strong enough to fully capture my interest.

A good, fun game, but not quite right for me.

RATING: 10
Why I think you should play this:

*Like Claustrophobia, the European influence to the theme makes it compelling for me. Somewhere between Terminator and Mad Max the factions find character. 

*The small board and colourful hexes keep the theme and present a unique aesthetic. I also enjoy the terrain add-ons in the first expansion and the Duel board for two-player games. 

*The asymmetry extends beyond simply the theming to very different styles of play for all of the armies (including some excellent fan expansions). Although the contrasts can produce disadvantage, it never leaves one army at an impossible vulnerability to a specific other.  

*For me, this game condenses what I liked about miniature wargaming (asymmetry, positioning, basic strategies, complex tactics) and eliminates what I didn't (expensive miniatures, unclear rules, customisation). That is plays in < 30 mins. is a big plus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A post-apocalyptic conflict game with some of the feel of an abstract; sacrifices, pinning and Kings (HQ) to be protected at all costs.  As this suggests, Neuroshima Hex isn't just a jolly shoot-em-up, but offers plenty of challenges to make the best use of your units.  

The four armies provided in the box are nicely varied, while the expansion Doomsday Machine are quirky fun.  Plays in 30 minutes with two.  My one four-player game ran much longer, but was still enjoyable, and it was nice to play on the full board.  

The rules seem complete, but are not the easiest to reference.  Production of the board and pieces is pretty good and the artwork is evocative.  I'd highly recommend downloading the army reference cards from BGG.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
El juego es rápido y entretenido. Nunca tienes tiempo de aburrirte ya que, incluso cuando no es tu turno, debes permanecer atento a los movimientos del resto de los jugadores. En este juego no existen las "interrupciones". Durante tu turno los otros jugadores no pueden modificar tus acciones con eventos y cartas especiales que te obliguen a cambiar de idea sobre lo que tienes que hacer.

El juego permite entre 1 y 6 jugadores (En la caja vienen 4 facciones básicas y una facción avanzada que, se supone, es algo más complicada de jugar). La modalidad de 1 jugador consiste en una baraja de cartas de desafío. Cada carta te asigna un objetivo que debes cumplir, te especifica la disposición inicial del tablero, las losetas que se pueden utilizar y el tiempo o movimientos requeridos para completarlo. La solución a cada desafío viene detrás de cada carta.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
For fans of abstract games and wargames, this is a nice mix.  Game play consists of playing unit tiles down in a hex grid.  Location and orientation matter, so players need to be thinking several turns ahead to what may come out next (for all players).  The game comes with 4 unique armies that can be played off each other.  The mix of these armies and the randomness of the tile draws ensures that the gameplay is different in each playing.  Normally, this variability is a strength in game, but with this one, the differences in armies results in a rather unusual set of learning curves (1 curve for each combination of armies),  Until all the armies are known and how they fare and match up against each other, the game can leave players feeling more played than player.  That said, if enough effort is made to understand the unique advantages and weaknesses of the forces then the game provides quite a bit of strategic options - all in a tight and quick playing package.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5

I like how each army has its own feel, and the random tile drawing might irritate some people, but I think it forces you to be more tactical.

First session.  The game is a very good 2 player game.  The team version works very well as well.  I like that each faction has a different feel to it and they are very balanced.  I have played as per rules of drawing 3 tiles and discard 1 every turn and I have also played with every tile is accessible from the get go. I suggest getting a chess clock since the game plays like chess in a way that every move has repercussions.

RATING: 7
A "miniatures" close combat game that is not bad at all. Your tiles (units) have various abilities that affect the (very small) field of battle, which is constantly changing. That's one of the things I didn't like, I'd like a larger grid with some permanent elements on it. The tiles that provide abilities to adjacent units could be made to withstand more hits and the board could be larger. Having said that, if you don't mind that the game is on the verge of being characterized as abstract, you'll enjoy it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
By far the most used game in my collection. Amazing tiles. Each faction is like a brand new game as each faction brings it's own abilities and uniqueness which will challenge even the seasoned of players. Tons of army expansions will keep you busy for years.

Analysis Paralysis can be tough. In some cases it's been down right ridiculous with this game. While they share many similarities all armies are specialized so it takes time to adjust to new abilities that may touch the board. Also, you can just throw out that 30min play time written on the box. I've played this game probably 3 dozen times or more from 2 player to 5 and I've only been able to wrap it up in 30mins just once. Normally, look for 1-2hrs(or more) depending on player count and familiarity with the game. 

The game is more than worth the buy. It REALLY shines once you start getting the expansion army factions.  The only down side with the game is the expansions come with papered instructions which can get funky looking after repeated folding and unfolding. My suggestion is to cut expansion instructions to size of base game box and laminate them for forever use.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played at BGG.Con and solo 2P once I purchased.

[b]Rules Summary[/b]
Objective: Destroy the other player's HQ or most detroyed by time tiles run out.

Shuffle tiles. Randomly pick start player. 1st turn draws one tile and either keeps discards or plays. 2nd turn draws two tiles and either keeps, discards, or plays either or both. 3rd turn draws three tiles and discards one, then keeps, discards, or plays up to two tiles. Each turn then draws up to three, discards one, then keeps, discards or plays two tiles.

Action tiles play immediately. Board tiles are played to the board.

Battles occur when Battle tile is played or all spaces filled. Battles by intiative starting with 4+ working down to 0.

[b]Initiative[/b] determines the order of battle and who gets to attack first. Units don't fight back they only attack and kill in initiative order.

[b]Toughness[/b] - can take wound marker, additional hits required for kill.

[b]Melee[/b] - attack adjacent
[b]Ranged[/b] - attack at distance over/through friendlies
[b]Armor[/b] - deflect 1 ranged damage
[b]Nets[/b] - immobilize adjacent units

RATING: 8.1
Bought from Games Chain in Chatswood; missing one commando token from Outpost (token was replaced quickly and easily, thanks to Z-Man).

Owned iPhone version before I bought physical copy - iPhone version sold me on this game, now I'm hooked - great abstract strategy game. Theme didn't really grab me, but the game underneath it is great. Ignore anyone who bitches about luck - the game is about fast gameplay and making the most of what you have. Sure, sometimes you'll lose becuase of a bad turn, but it's pretty rare, and most of the time you'll be at fault, not the draw deck. Ultimately the length of time between battles ameliorates any bad luck turn-to-turn - you always have two or three draws between battles and the game is about initiative, not draws.

I love how well-balanced all the armies are - I have won and lost with all of them in all sorts of matchups. Great game, can't see myself ever getting bored of it. Can't wait to get expacks with new "armies".

Got rid of pre-Melbourne move

RATING: 10
[video=62458][/video]
[video=63258][/video] [video=64923][/video] [video=65184][/video] [video=75893][/video]

Neuroshima Hex is an abstract tactical combat game (think chess) set in a post apocalypse wasteland spread out on a Hex Grid 5 spaces across (7 spaces for a 5 or 6 player game).  Players take turns placing their Base on the Grid and throughout the game they take turns placing troops, support units or triggering actions until the grid is full.  Then in descending order of initiative the units will take shots at each other or fight in hand to hand combat.  Slowly they eliminate each other until the grid is mostly empty and start all often again.  The last player standing, or the player whose base has the most of their 20 life points remaining, is declared the winner.

What makes Neuroshima Hex so interesting is the Armies.  Each comes with 35 tiles and is unique in their composition and combination of units, including a unique player power.  The uniqueness of each army contributes greatly to their overall strategy and players really get a feel for the style they are geared for once the units come out.  One army may be a marauding band of hand-to-hand brutes while the other may be a mobile strike force of specialists.  These strategies really start change up once you increase the player count from your initial two.  The opponents will get more opportunities to exploit your armies weakness while in the cramped quarters you will get more opportunities to maximise your unique capabilities.  

Neuroshima Hex changes from a strategic came to a tactical masterpiece once you set up however.  After placing your base you will shuffle the remaining tiles face down and you're at the mercy of the draw.  Even worse, out of the three titles you draw each turn you must throw away one before you place the other two.  This really leads players to decide best possible action, sometimes when none is optimal.

Despite the agony of choice and the seeming cruelty  of the draw, Neuroshima Hex never feels luck driven and is incredibly well balanced.  Once you go down the rabbit hole of expansion armies, you'll find endless replayability and tactical joy.

RATING: 6
I find this to be clever fun. It's short, constrained, and fast-moving, which I like. The armies have good variety. Different matchups play very differently. If you avoid the free-for-all games like the plague (if you must play multi-player, play 2-on-2 teams), there is fun to be had here.

My only real reservation about it is that it can be a bit of an analysis trap. Because of the randomness of the tile draws, I'm not sure a huge amount of analysis is rewarded, but some definitely is, and with combat being deterministic and all it's a bit prone to absorbing more energy than the game is really worth. Like Carcasonne, I think it's lighter than it actually looks or plays.

But, if you can avoid that and keep the game time down to 20-30 minutes, and keep the pace going, this is good fun I think. Not a top-tier type, but fills a niche.

RATING: 4
Updated 5/29/10.

I've exclusively played this game 2-player, in the event that provides some explanation for the low rating, but this one just doesn't really work for me.  For a game about futuristic cyber-wars, the gameplay is too analytical, and the resolution of battles is too mechanical.  It literally put my opponent to sleep on the last play.  

The whole thing feels much more like a puzzle than like a game with any sort of flow.  I never had the sense of accomplishment or progression, in large part because the game lacks the capacity for long-term planning or for any sort of dedicated path-dependent strategies.  The random tile draw feels extremely limiting, perhaps because the tiles have such wildly different effects.  By contrast, the two games that I would most compare Neuroshima Hex to are Attika and Samurai.  I rate both those games significantly higher because the disparities between the benefits of each randomly-drawn tile are minimized, the decision of when or where to play randomly drawn tiles seems to have greater strategic significance, and the game (usually) requires progressing through all the tiles, so that lucky draws are counterbalanced.  

In short, Neuroshima Hex isn't a terrible game (I didn't want to gouge my eyes out), but its a game with dreary gameplay that stands in stark contrast to its dynamic theme, with mechanics that feel artificially constraining and uninspiring, and with considerably better competition in the genre.  Decidedly mediocre.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Rated "8" after one play, and this will end up anywhere from a 6 to a 10. I won't know until I become more familiar with the strategy. The symbology is not as tough as, say, Race for the Galaxy, but there's a minor learning curve with the powers and abilities of each piece.

Also, strategy is not immediately apparent. I don't think I'll have a firm grasp on how to strategically play until after 5+ games. However, this appears to be a surprisingly deep, strategic game. I'm intrigued, and I want to learn more. I'm especially curious to try team play with four players.

Update: played this again recently, and looking at my original comments, I don't  think I can say it's as deep as I first suspected, but that doesn't mean I like it less. There's plenty to study, but there's luck of the draw. If it lasted 2 hours that would be frustrating. But for 20 to 30 minutes, I view it more as a fun and aggressive "super filler."

Update: played this the first time as a four-player team game. It was not as cluttered and confusing as I thought it might be. I have mixed feelings about this game. On the one hand, it is definitely unique. But there's a few strikes against it: the symbology is tough to pick up, so the game demands a number of plays. It's probably best as a two player game, and the luck of the draw can be pretty brutal... bad enough that you're left with no real decision on your turn. I can't figure out if this is trade bait or not.

Update: well, the answer ended up as "trade bait." I traded this in a math trade for Samurai. I can see where others will grow to really like this game. But for me, the fact that it is really best with two, with a steep learning curve of iconography, and a noticeable randomness of the draw... I just got other games I'd rather be playing.

RATING: 2
This game is essentially a "snowball fight with modifiers" with the potential for more than two players to participate. The result is a game that is essentially going to produce as the winner the player who got picked on the least - not necessarily the player who planned the best. If you don't mind that kind of game then go for it and play this game. I personally don't find that kind of dynamic in a game very rewarding and I most likely won't be playing this again as I find no intrinsic satisfaction in a victory earned simply because I was ignored enough by the other players. 

Even if I were to play a two player game where problems with triangulation are not an issue, the luck of the draw is strong enough in the game's design to cause me to want to rely on a thematic experience for satisfaction instead of on the game's mechanical aspects. However, this game is more of a collection of abstract mechanics rather than a thematic set of mechanics designed around telling an interesting story. The result is that I probably won't play this ever again unless it's a two player game and, even then, I'm not inclined to play it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Its a colorful game, with an interesting theme...but it comes off feeling very much like an abstract game. That's not a knock, but is a bit of a mismatch with the thematic battle game the box makes you expect.

I've only played twice, but I would tweak the rules SLIGHTLY in a way that I think improves the game. I would distinguish the tactics and units on the back of the tiles. You'd essentially be deciding which of two piles to draw from for any given tile draw.

In each game, we had situations where a player drew all tactics and could literally do nothing but discard on his turn. With the way the endgame tempo occurs, its a pretty large disadvantage not to be able to do anything on a turn. 

The different armies are interesting. I've no idea if they're balanced. The game plays quickly enough, and works well for what it is. Its reminiscent of Vortex, although more streamlined. Some people will prefer the special abilities of the Vortex tiles more, but I like the simplicity and finite nature of NH better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Love this tile laying war game with an SF theme.  It feels so hopeless as you battle to damage your opponent's home base more than they can damage yours.  Each set of tiles represents an army.  Each tile has various powers, some of which face only in certain directions.  Tough decisions abound as you decide which tiles to keep and which to discard out of the game, where to place a tile, and which direction to face it.  Battles can be forced by playing a tile or occur automatically if the board is full.  Tiles killed in battle are removed from the board, freeing up space to lay more tiles until the next battle.  Replayability is great as each army is different with its own powers and strategies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
-.5 for the box with the bottom half completely sealed off and hollow. Are publishers really that desperate to try to make their games stand out? It's really a shame for those of us who value shelf space more than retail presence.

I'm really enjoying this one. A few plays in and I'm not sure whether all sides are balanced, but some of the imbalances we thought we saw turned out to be completely wrong. We thought Blue was the best and Green the worst, until I just destroyed Blue with Green. It has just the right amount of theme, but really it's pretty abstract. You places units on the board, and they don't do anything until a battle happens, which is about once every few turns. The board is mostly static except for a few units which can move, and a move tile which les you move a unit. For sure there is a lot of luck, though, and probably 80% is tactics, and only 20% strategy beyond current turn. It's about being opportunistic and grabbing a few hits of the opponent's base when you can get them, even if you need to sacrifice units in the process. As someone mentioned to me when I was considering this, this works well with people who like gipf games, but want more theme, and are ok adding some luck of the draw into the mix.

RATING: 8
A game of positional advantage distilled down... I can't say "to its fundamental essence", because that would be Go, but down to a fairly concentrated brew, abstracting away the majority of things you see in wargames. (This is war-themed, but not a a wargame - unless you count Go as a wargame, in which case it totally is.)

I quite like it - the spatial / timing combination of battle resolution appeals very strongly to me, and manipulating it can be a great deal of fun. I suspect that the luck of the tile draws will start to irritate me as I play more, but for now I'm enjoying it too much to care. (Besides, house-ruling for more tile control looks easy.)

Gamers I trust have told me to never, ever play this 3-player, and to play 4-player only as 2v2 teams. I can believe it.

RATING: 4
I've played this 2-player, 3-player, 4-player, and 4-player (teamed) with a lot of different race combos.  The verdict: this game is seriously overrated and overpriced.  3-player is awful (gang up on the loser), 2-player is somewhat okay, 4-player is a mess, and 4-player teamed is simply frustrating because of friendly fire rules.  

Incidentally, those rules make certain team combos way unbalanced, and the only way to beat them is to hope that team gets a bad tile draw. Random tile draws make the decisions, not you. 

Summary: If you're tempted to buy this because you're thinking 'How can a top 200 board game be bad?', don't.  It's bad.

iOS Version:  I'd give the iPhone version a 7 though, because of no AP and challenging AI players.  

UPDATE: Honestly, if you just accept the fact that it's pure tactics, it's not terrible considering how quickly it plays.  Still bad; just not terrible.

RATING: 9
Multiplayer is the very definition of bringing lightsabers to a knife fight in a phone booth. Absolutely chaotic and fun, I definitely prefer scoring based on damage to other HQs rather than current HQ health when playing multiplayer. 2P is a highly strategic game, the disparity of powers and abilities that each army has balances surprisingly well. Mephisto and Dancer are a blast to play in 2P, very much unlike the other armies. Own all English printed armies, not sure if I should wait for English reprints of the remaining armies or just buy Polish versions and print out the English rules. I like having a consistent print quality with expansion army rules.

RATING: 6
After one play.

This game is a real brain burner in some respects.  It definitely would take multiple plays to develop a cohesive strategy.  Throughout the game, we missed several things due to the shear amount of information (and rule exceptions) that are on the board at any one time.  I would play again, but there are large number of games that I would choose well before this one.

I love the fact that there are many different armies that have asymmetric abilities.  The game does a good job to forewarn you of the strategy you might want to employ given the skills of you units.  The components are nice, and the board allows variability in size.  Although, the different color spaces are hard to remember sometimes.  There are a few confusing mistakes in the rule book, but nothing that is not logically rectified.  

If theme is a huge deal to you, this game does not quite deliver.  The theme, while it works well, does not have any real connection to the mechanics besides a little bit of flavor.

RATING: 9
[2015-04-28 Rating: 7.5 -> 9]
Even after all this years, Neuroshima Hex still feels fresh. Whenever the game hits the table (or my tablet, which seems to be the more common scenario these days), I'm surprised how different it is from all other games I've played.

The available armies are all very well balanced although it might not appear so on the first few plays. While there are some rather straightforward ones, most of the more advanced armies from the expansions (like NeoJungle or Smart) need a few learning games before you get an (rough) idea on how to play with or against them. But that is part of the fun and maybe one of the few drawbacks of the game: It's best if you have at least one dedicated opponent with whom you can delve into the strategic depth of this game. Then (and only then), Neuroshima Hex is a blast!

(PS: I've only played Neuroshima Hex with two players and can't comment on any other player count.)

[2011-02-12 Rating: n.a. -> 7.5]
This one is a very clever tile-laying game. Full of decisions but thanks to the random draw of tiles, it isn't overwhelming.
The armies all play very differently. Some are stronger in melee, others kill better from the distance and some specialize in disabling the opponent's units.
While building up your army on the player board, there is a lot of tension and when it comes to fighting, there will be a lot of casulties.

RATING: 7
I have heard this described as "a knife fight in a phone booth."  While that is an apt description, it's only partially correct. 

I think the best way to imagine this game is to visualize the mousetrap experiment they use to help kids understand nuclear fission - take a room full of mousetraps, and set a ping pong ball on each one, then toss in another ball.  In just a short moment, almost all the traps have been set off, and all the balls are bouncing around everywhere.  Then, just reset and repeat the process.  That's this game in a nutshell.  Chaotic, brutal, and very very fun.

RATING: 5
I'm going to buck the trend with this game in my rating.  I think it's below average in the amount of depth that it gives for the number of rules you have to learn.  I've played over 10 times and I still have to consult the manual almost every game to remind myself what some of the units do.

I've only played 2 player games on the iPhone app, so perhaps that explains my contrary experience.  I'm also not a fan of exceptions, and it feels like this game has a lot of exceptions (well, they are simply different types of units, but there are a lot of different types, and the symbols are not intuitive - they just have to be memorized).  The game is also a bit of a puzzle game, which is one of the types of games that I dislike.  While it's not difficult to figure the puzzle out, you have to take a little bit of time to think it through, especially to make sure you have not forgotten how oneof hte symbols works.

--- edit ---
I have now played the game as a multi-player game, and it is MUCH better.  Instead of a below average rating of a 3.75, I'd give it an above average rating of a 6.25.  So I am rating it a 5, the average of my experience with it as a two player game and my experience with it as a muti-player game.

--- edit ---
I raised my rating to a 6 (which is pretty darn good for how hard I rate games).  I still don't like this as a two player game, but there is something very fun about the multi-player version.  It's got a nice mix of strategy and luck.   

--- edit ---
Played it face to face, and I'm returning my rating to a 5.  I like the computer version better because the program figures out and resolves the puzzles (I mean battles).

RATING: 9
Players play a futuristic faction, battling with their forces on a tiny hex based board.  Players take turns to draw back to a hand of three hex tiles from their pool of 35.  Tiles can be saved, discarded or played onto the tiny 19 space game board.  Tiles are split into "forces" and "actions".  Forces take their place on the board, actions do stuff with the forces.  The forces have several attributes - facing, type of attack, initiative, toughness, etc, and can also be modified by "modules" which connect to them.  When a battle triggers, it's all resolved in order of initiative - totally luck free (well, if you forget the tile draw aspect).  The winner is the player who deals the most damage to their opponents HQ.  The four factions that are included in the game are nice and different.  Bold symbols (apart from armour, which is easy to miss) make everything clear.  Great little game - only played two player so far.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Another in the line of 'intriguing games that I don't play enough of.' Through my understanding of the game mechanics, I would only ever play this 2-player, due to the small map size and how very cramped it already feels playing 2-player.

I adore this idea in combat - players set up units, aiming in particular directions, with different priority and effectiveness, but not acting yet. Then, at some point, on player drops the token which instigates battle and everything breaks loose. The units are decimated and only a few tend to remain standing through the absurd crossfire. A lot of fun, I probably just need to get more games under my belt to offer a deeper opinion.

RATING: 4
Hex based tactical warfare.  Sounds good but I would rather play Maelstrom/Vortex.  With only 30 tiles available in the game (and only a little more than half of them are able to attack), the luck of the draw seems to have a large effect on the outcome.  Example: Let's say in my first 3 - 4 turns I draw two combat units whereas my opponent draws about 4 - 5, that puts me at a major disadvantage because my units will likely die in the first battle and it's possible that none of my opponents would if he had a chance to lay some of those five units after I placed my two.  In future rounds, I would now have all of those units to deal with and only limited/suicidal spaces to lay my own units.  This happened to me on my first game.  It was annoying to lose in this manner and even if it happened to my opponent rather than me, I would feel no accomplishment in winning.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Neuroshima Hex may be the greatest light-strategy game to ever exist.  The game is incredibly simple but don't let that fool you as tactically it has a lot of depth and strategy that; while the game has elements of luck and chance, typically Neuroshima Hex doesn't weigh too heavily upon them.  The game also has a wide array of expansion armies to expand the already very high replayability.

Games are fairly quick, and work on ramping tension, quoting Zee Garcia, "It's like a knife fight in a phone booth."

I will add a caveat, this game is really a two-player game despite what the box says.

Neuroshima Hex is solid strategy combined with instant gratification in a box (or on a mobile device).


+ Neuroshima Hex! New York
+ Neuroshima Hex! Steel Police
+ Neuroshima Hex! Uranopolis

RATING: 6.5
Not exactly my cup of tea, but it works very well for what it is. The very small battle area made positioning crucial, and since the tile draw is random, getting the wrong tiles at the start can be a big limitation. I can see why people enjoy this game though, and the potential in the system is high, so it could develop its niche in the market. The graphics are okay, the rules are well enough written, but the printing was a little poor. The graphics are offset on the tiles, so higher quality control is needed. The game system could lend itself to a boardgame version of Tower Of Defense games.

RATING: 8
No jogo nos controlamos facções que tem caraterísticas ligeiramente diferentes.
Basicamente, em cada jogada você coloca dois de três peças (tiles) que tem variadas funções e habilidades que funcionam isolada e combinadamente. Basicamente, estas funções tentam eliminar os tiles dos outros jogadores para conseguir infligir danos nas bases de cada jogador. Fantástico!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
(Rating for 2 player game.) Very enjoyable tactical 2-player battle game with a quick playing time. Luck of the draw play an important role, but you still have to carefully place your fighters. It's not a brainburner, so it can act as a quick filler. Finally, the diversity of the deck means you have to adapt your strategy to the group you're playing. I didn't play enough games to comment on balance.

(As a side note, it somewhat reminds me of a side-game that was included in the PS2 title Yuyu Hakusho Dark Tournament, for which I was a programmer. Needless to say, Neuroshima Hex is light-years ahead.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Well, for me it is still most likely one of the best games that came from Poland and it's a boardgame I am pretty much always happy to play. That explains the high rating, really - just tell me to play it, and I will!

It's thrilling, quick, full of tension and the theme is very well suited to a game of brutal fights, where tokens and tactics goes together in a surprisingly clever and deep strategy game. Sure, luck is still a factor here, and yes, some armies are a bit better or worse against certain opponents, but the game is still well balanced for such asymmetry in the factions and thanks to the rich amount of expansions, quick gameplay and adrenaline pumping action its replayability is amazing.

+Steel Police
+Dancer
+Sharrash
+Mephisto
+Mississippi
+Uranopolis
+Death Breath
+Doomsday Machine
+Assassin
+Mad Bomber

PROS:
-Equilibra de forma excelente el uso de la estrategia con la táctica
-Todos los ejércitos están muy bien equilibrados y cada uno tiene su propia estrategia
-El efecto del azar es muy limitado
-Cuenta con una historia detrás muy elaborada
-Permite el uso de piezas especiales (Assassin y Mad Bomber) que cambian el transcurso del juego
-Fácil de aprender, de montar y de recoger
-Duración ni muy larga ni muy corta
-Aunque no hay edición en español, la dependencia del idioma es nula

CONTRAS:
-A 3, 5 y 6 jugadores no funciona bien
-Es necesaria experiencia con el ejército con el que se juega para poder dominar la partida

RATING: 3
Played a couple of times, thought it might get better with familiarity. Played it a couple more times, and can't see playing it again unless I was indulging someone.

The problem is simple: There are two types of tiles in the game (well, technically there are three, but two are grouped together in the rules...) 'board tiles' and 'action tiles'. The 'board tiles' are the ones you need, they represent 'units' and 'modules'. *Only units can attack!* So if one player is drawing 'modules' and 'action tiles' while the other is drawing 'units', guess who wins? 

Waaaaay too random for me.

RATING: 8
Neuroshima Hex plays 2-4 players, but I've only played with 2 and it seems like it's probably best in that configuration (total guess).  The interesting thing about this game is that the battle resolution is totally deterministic. When a battle happens, you progress through numbered initiative phases. Certain characters strike or fire in specified directions on a specified phase. But it they're knocked out before that phase happens, they'll never get to attack. There are also character boosting tiles called "modules," and various fast effect tiles. What makes this tightly scheduled battle system work is the way that characters and other tiles are randomly drawn each turn. Even the timing of the battles is controlled by specific "battle" tiles that give players the option of initiating a battle when drawn.

The victory conditions themselves also add an interesting tension to the game. Each player controls a powerful headquarters unit, and the player who inflicts the most damage on the enemy headquarters wins the game. Things develop such that players often have to choose between controlling the board on one hand, and attacking the enemy headquarters on the other hand. One usually comes at the expense of the other.

RATING: 7
A solid combative abstract-ish game. Lots of replay in the box and a bunch of expansions to get into too. Game itself feels quite well tuned and tactical. 

Different starter factions are really well balanced and the 3 player is just as fun as the two for me. Mechanically the game is surprisingly intuitive and can be well understood after a couple of rounds. Trying to make efficient piece trades while setting up to hit the enemy base makes for a high-tactics feel with a bit of strategy in trying to setup certain arrangements of pieces. Pace is nice and the swings between the setup of pieces and battles means there are plenty of little victories (and defeats) that keep the game tense. 

"Drawing" tiles annoys me, so hard to shuffle them. Probably going to switch up how I manage that in the future (Bag to draw from?).

RATING: 6
Very novel tile-laying game. The special powers of the tiles aren't difficult, but the interactions of modules and bases get tricky; as a result, the game feels less streamlined than it ought to. It's very much a puzzle kind of game, and I still like it despite the fact that I don't enjoy puzzles.

RATING: 7
I really want to get more, and not just solo, plays out of this one. Amazing mechanics, very simple and fast gameplay, lots and lots of tactical options and tons of decisions to make.

Even when I first bought the game, I underestimated it a bit. There's a lot of depth in the different armies, and fast armies (Outpost) play very different from hard-hitting slow ones (Moloch).

What's great as well is that the hexes have different meanings, are either for placing on the board or for adding effects like movement or battles - this makes every army a kind of deck that can be played more or less efficiently.

The fast play time makes it a lot less important that occasionally, battles are decided by lucky draws. All the more reason for just one more game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Complaints that this game is abstract and puzzly are confused and misguided. There is a tremendous amount of action and excitement, twists and reversals, packed into this little game. Games are over in 30-45 minutes, but they are very intense, absorbing and layered. The build up and resolution of battles makes for a very dynamic game.  While the battle resolution is diceless, it still offers some surprises as the board changes with each step in the battle. Best of all, each army plays completely differently. This is asymmetrical gameplay at its finest, and it guarantees endless replayability. Awesome, awesome game. Babel 13 is a fantastic expansion.

The more I play this, the more I like it. Easy to teach, extremely replayable, can fit in to almost any game night schedule, and very unique.

RATING: 7
6-12-10
Updated rating from a 5 to a 7 after playing a couple two-player games.  My first impression was pretty sour on this game, feeling it to be too random and ultimately a crapshoot on who won.  You were just reacting to the board, not doing much thinking about where to place stuff other than 'protect self, hurt others'.

I still feel it is too random.  If one player chooses a hand of all specials and no units, he is at a huge disadvantage.  The hex draws are very unforgiving and there is no way to mitigate your risk of random draws.  It also still feels reactive but the more times you play through, you can get a feel for what units will work where and what synergies you may encounter.

Overall it still doesn't wow me, but it's a fun little game that plays relatively quickly at a fairly low level of complexity.

RATING: 6.1
Aug08: Surprisingly fun little game here!  It's got plenty of conflict, but it's pretty quick to resolve and fun to boot.  The rules flex the same muscles as Race for the Galaxy.  If you can quickly grasp the way "Consume" works in RftG, then resolving battles in Neuroshima should be easy for you.

I also like the tough decisions you are faced with every turn.  You have to through out a tile every turn, and you just never want to.  You need to go on the attack and also on the defense, and it's just tough to do both.

I don't think the included armies are all that balanced, but they sure do offer gabs of replayability.  It also seems to play better with the fewer number of people.

I also suggest waiting for the Z-Man import.  The current publication has a few usability issues that hopefully Zev will clean up.

Aug09: Note to self.  Wife hates this game.

RATING: 10
An eminently repayable light war-game, very tactical and reasonably strategic. One might consider it rather like chess, only instead of setting the pawns and pieces out in a specified fashion, players take turns drawing a few at random and placing them wherever they like. Surprisingly deep, with importance placed on orientation of the hexagonal tiles; but the random draws and multi-stage combat can make it difficult to reason about (IMO).

I own the boxed game, but I’ve played the wonderful iOS version many more times. The boxed game has the drawback of rather low build quality — the pieces are cardboard, many of them are printed off center, and it was easy to lightly damage a few of them when poking them out of the sheets they came on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
A Polish post-apocalyptic game.  As of this writing, I have played it twice.  Once a 4-player team game, the other as a 1-on-1 game with 2 players.  They play very different but are both enjoyable.  The four player was more random, while the two player was a little more strategic.  

This is a game that should be played quickly and will really suffer from analysis paralysis.  Players who feel the need to compute every possible variation for their moves will drag this game down quickly.

RATING: 8
The first couple of times I played this game, I thought: "Why the hype?  It's so random."  But the more I played, the more I realized how the mechanisms and components work together to create something that is as much strategy as luck-based. Certainly your carefully orchestrated attack can be destroyed by your opponent drawing the right tile at the right time, but overall, knowing your army's strengths and weaknesses, knowing how often certain tiles are likely to appear (like roulette), and making intelligent decisions are just as important in factoring who wins and loses.  A champion shouldn't be decided on the basis of one game, but a best of seven would certainly indicate the better player.  The clincher I think is the length.  Playing two games in 60 minutes is not unreasonable for two people, meaning quick, tactical fun with deceivingly simple decisions required on every turn. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

2011-07-19

Initial Rating: 8.0 (March 2010)

I really REALLY like Neuroshima Hex! One of the first games I played and one of my all time favorites. I generally don’t like chaos games (I can’t plan ahead so it’s mostly luck), so I think this game is great only for 2 players (preferred option) or 4 players in teams, otherwise it leads to kingmaking and the player who stays out of the fight wins easily.

The components and the art are high quality, the time play is relatively short and each unit offers a wide spectrum of tactics. Also the theme is awesome!!! Best Polish game ever. 

Nowadays I play this a lot more on the Ipad than the physical version. The iPad makes it so easy to play as it handles the bits automatically :D.

Current Rating: 8.5
[hr]
2015-09-07

After more than 5 years of continued play, I still enjoy Neuroshima Hex! a lot. I have bought everything I could for the physical and the online game and I always want to play it. I love that the game still gets support and new expansions every year.

Neuroshima Hex! has a feature many of my favorite games share as well (Dungeon Twister, Summoner Wars, Dominion). In each game you only play with a small subset of the possible armies (4 with the base game, much more with the expansions) adding a lot of depth and replayability with very few rules overhead.

I’m not sure I like the new 3.0 art, buts that’s maybe because I was accustomed to the old one, however on the iPad you cannot choose the old art and it is a shame. 

On the bad side, I recently bought the 3.0 Portal edition just to found out that the Doomsday Machine army wasn’t included in it, it is included only in the Z-Man edition. I felt cheated because this information isn’t widely available AND I exclusively bought that edition of the game for that army. They should make this a lot clearer. I already sold my copy of the game and I kept the 2.5 edition.

Current Rating: 8.5
[hr]
2016-01-01

I recently played a face to face Neursohima Hex! Game and I felt it dragged a bit. I’m spoiled because I use the iPad version all the time. The only occasions I want to play face to face is when using additional rules that aren’t available in the app (energy, teams, campaign) or with armies not yet in the app.

Due to this, I’m downgrading its rating, but I still feel it is a tremendous game and I still have in in my game collection.

Current Rating: 7.5

RATING: 7
Played this for the first time in Aug. 2009. I've wanted to try it for a very long time, but the timing just didn't work out till now. I enjoyed it.

We played twice. So this rating and reaction isn't locked in yet.

The first game was a 4 player game and we got some big rules wrong. We worked them out as we played forward, a bit messy, but it worked. I won. The rules we learned in the play (now that we have them right) make a huge difference. 

The 2 player game that followed was with Dan. I pounded him in this go. It was a slaughter, but it was fun. I'd definetly play it again and each exposure would make you better at it.

What to think of the game? 

I enjoyed it over all. 

It had the feel of a collectible miniature's battle game (like Warcraft) without the collectability. 

There is a lot of tactical play with the tile draws, but there is also real strategy in knowing your troop count, abilities, and where you position and plan. I seemed to grok it very well and quickly.

I seemed to really enjoy it actually. I'll have to pick it up I think.

I'm concerned a bit with the tile draws being to impactful to play, but it didn't actually bother me in the playing even when I drew poorly, but it could kill you if your HQ gets pounded down before you get units on the board.

In the four player game, I could tell Kevin was going to come back as he had many units still undrawn and the end game would be close. He didn't see that at first and thought he was getting pounded, but his HQ had not taken enough damage to drive it down.

I won that game by one point. It was a good rebuild for Kevin and with Dan and him coming at me, it ended up being a good fight (Adrian got pasted early, but it was due to the missed rules and not his fault).

I like the no luck combat, the various phases of initiative and the bonus tiles. 

The iconography, much like Race for the Galaxy, works, but takes a bit to learn. Once you learn it, it seems straight forward.

RATING: 7
An engaging little wargamey abstract (or abstractish wargame?) played out on a small tactical board of hexes. Four factions are available that all play with subtly different types of units. Each player defends a home base and deploys units that can do ranged or melee attacks--and receive strength/initiative bonuses from other adjacent tiles. Periodically battles break out that are resolved quite simply in order from highest initiative units to lowest. It has a moderate initial learning curve, but plays fast and offers plenty of good decisions for its heft. 

My rating is based on the genius official iTouch app available for the game.

RATING: 7
A combat game fought by randomly drawing army and action tiles and placing them on a tightly confined hexagonal grid. When a battle action is played, or the board is full, battle ensues. The game is entirely tactical. Players position the tiles they draw as effectively as possible. The base game comes with four armies that have different strengths and weaknesses, and more are available through expansions. The random draw ensures that battles between the same two armies never unfold the same way twice and increases re-playability. There is enough difference between the army units so that the considerations taken when playing a tile are fresh with each placement.

If this game were any longer, I think the random draw would bother me. But the 30 minute play time (up to an hour if you have slow players) is perfect for the kind of game this is. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This game does a lot with such a simple mechanism. Tons of replay value and interesting variant options. Battle resolution can be bothersome when everyone tries to put their hands on the board.

RATING: 10
Post-Apocalyptic Ameritrash Mexican-Standoff Chess.

It's got that "if I take you, then you take me, then I take you..." dynamic that occurs with captures in Chess, but, here it's on steroids.

Loved the post-apocalyptic theme and back story. This is a hex-based spatial battle game with an appealing mix of strategy and tactics, randomness and careful planning, which feels very thematic. Each army is asymmetric, so it's fun to discover the strengths and weaknesses of your particular army. It's got an interesting rhythm of "pulses" of bloody battles throughout the game. We played with 4; because it's kind of chess-like, I think it probably would play best with 2, due to downtime.

Note: 3rd ed. has solo variant!!!

UPDATE: having tried the Android App, 2-player against AI, it plays much better 2-player.  Upgrading from 8.5 to 9.5.  Awesome game!

UPDATE:
Upgrading from 9.5 to 10, and this is going in my Top 10.  Brilliant game.  The expansions completely change the strategy and are fun to explore, like Neojungle & The Dancer.

RATING: 10
I love this game.  Mechanically simple, strategically deep with just enough random chance that a relatively new player can stand a chance against a more experienced player (especially in multiplayer).

Variant rules in the box AND an extra (slightly overpowered[Doomsday Machine]) race available in the box, so you get a LOT of value out of the starter set alone.

I have very little negative critique for this game, occasionally mother fate can see it fit to screw you over, this can be remedied by a slight modification on the draw rules for more experienced players (to ensure less random and more strategy).

This is one of the most succinct games around and there is next to no setup time and gameplay is short (don't recommend 4 player as it starts to drag.

I would buy this & all the expansions if I had the free money, space, time to play and friends to play it with regularly! :)

RATING: 9
One of the best games I've played. It is fast paced with quite a lot of thinking. Not much of planning actually, more like adjusting to the current situation on the board and taking advantage of drawn tiles. Rules are simple but offer endless possibilities.

The biggest downside of the game is randomness. It can lead even the best player to losing the game.

It's designed for two players and works best for two. Three player game often leads to kingmaking. Four player game in teams 2vs2 can be fun from time to time, especially if one of the players has Moloch army.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
No matter how many times I played it I could never get into this game. I understand the puzzle-like aspect of the game: that you are in this chaotic firefight in a small arena-like area and you never know what is going to happen next. But for me, the random tile draw killed the game for me, because it makes the game too much like Klondike solitaire. There is little sense of strategy here, and NH is obviously intended to be a strategy game. Like cooperative games, how you do depends almost entirely upon what you draw, and I don't like that.

As the game continues, you expect the tension to mount, but as the board fills up you actually have fewer and fewer choices, so it is obvious what you should do each turn. Ultimately, the fact that you cannot choose when your units will fire their weapons killed it for me. You have all these badass units in a rich post-apocalyptic setting and yet for the most part they just stand there.

I like the idea of setting up your units in a confined space, and the units here are very well done, but what you do with them (or, rather, what you can't do with them) makes this game fall flat with me.

Chyba najbardziej znana gra Michała Oracza. Kilkanaście różnicowanych gatunków pszczół a jeden ul do zdobycia. Zwykle walczą na raz dwa gatunki, na przemian atakują swoich rywali i ich królową. Grzeczne są i karne bo każdy czeka na swoją kolej by użądlić w zależności od poziomu swojej inicjatywy. Wojownicy mają zróżnicowane umiejętności i bronie, niektórzy mogą atakować w kilku kierunkach, niektórzy tylko osłaniają lub paraliżują przeciwników. Brak trutni. Nikt tu nikogo nie zapyla. ;) Dwa nowe gatunki już powstają w laboratoriach czyli MOracz pracuje nad dwoma nowymi frakcjami - jedna może nawet w tym roku na Essen.
Sprytna i bardzo popularna gra, szkoda że w klimatach postapo. Równie dobrze mogło by być o magach w świecie fantasy, o postaciach z bajek robiących sobie psikusy walcząc o tort, bakteriach chcących zdobyć DNA komórki lub jak wyżej dzikie pszczoły. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love this game!
Nice and short, easy to pick up (May take one game to fully understand)  Simple strategic placement of units to battle it out, team up or do a free for all.
Simply put, all units have an initiative, and attack in turn order.  If someone places a unit attacking your base, you can place a faster unit attacking his unit.  In turn you can kill his unit before it attacks your base.
But battles don't happen that fast.  Usually he will counter you by putting a unit to attack your unit that is attacking his, that is attacking your base!  Get all that? :D
Good cause that's just the tip of the iceberg

RATING: 5
Aggressive tile laying game in which you are seeking to take out your opponents' base/units.  Some tiles are units with varying abilities and some tiles refer to instantaneous events such as battle, move, grenade, airstrike and the like.  Each of the four armies have different units which will force you to implement different strategies in most instances.  Interesting concept for gameplay, I just didn't really enjoy the game so much.  Game play is much better if you can play with a knowledge of what strengths and weaknesses each of the different armies have.

RATING: 7
[u]Comments[/u]
Is Poland the gaming heaven? This is the second game from Portal Publishing that has been excellent. I wanted this game for so long, and it didn't disappoint. 

[u]Updated Comments:[/u]
After trading away Neuroshima Hex a number of years ago I recently got a chance to give it another go, and I am really glad I did. The rules are tight, and the gameplay fast and furious, and easy to understand. NH is still one of the most tactical games you can find in the non-wargame space for boardgames. The new armies definitely add to the complexity and the replayability of the game. The components are pretty well made, although a better graphic designer could really have made this game really pop. Although I really enjoyed my game of NH, I don't feel as though I need to have the game in my collection.

RATING: 9
One of my all-time favorite games.  It takes the abstract strategy of Hive and muddies it up with variable powers, and a dash of luck, which takes the pressure off some people.  When you lose in Hive, it's because YOU lost.  When you lose in Neuroshima, there's a good chance you were outplayed, but there's also the possibility you had some bad luck.

Each faction plays/feels different, some of which are so different that it almost feels like a different game.  Each requires different strategies to play/play against, creating TONS of variability with the total amount of factions.

RATING: 10
A great tactical battle game that does not strike me as being nearly as abstract as I heard it described.  Definitely has the feel of a knife fight in a phonebooth at times.  If I am looking for a tactical conflict game, I would play this in place of C&C games, because you could play NH twice before you even set up the scenario for C&C.  This has replaced LotR:tC as my two-player quick conflict game of choice.  The mechanic of  essentially combining gameplay with setup is done brilliantly.  The tile draws and no-luck-combat are just the right mix of luck and strategy to keep maximum tension going through the game.  This really is a two-player game.  I have not and do not plan on trying multi-player much, as it seems it is prone to frustrating AP and two-on-one situations.  

Update:  I am hooked!  Looking forward to trying out fan-generated armies and whatever new ones Portal is putting out.

RATING: 9
Man do I love this game. Before trying it I heard it described two ways: as a knife fight in a phone both, and as being like filling up a room with mousetraps and then tossing in a ball. Both analogies are apt, brilliant, and entirely fitting.

With very low rules overhead and a short play time, NH crams a ton of tough decision-making, just the right amount of luck, a great deal of variety, lots of tension, novel gameplay, and a ton of fun into a very, very tight package. It's a thing of beauty. It's also easy to learn and easy to play without lacking strategic depth.

My only niggling complaint is that the board is too busy. I wish the game came with an alternate board of the minimum size necessary for a two-player game.

RATING: 5
3 / 5 - Good.

I can see where the fun is in this - there's a good balance between the random nature of the tile draw and good strategy and tactics in using what you've drawn. However the entirely deterministic nature of the tile effects is hugely off-putting - trying to work out what will affect what when both during planning and battles is a pain in the arse. I'm also turned off by the somewhat abstract nature of the game: there's no narrative or sense of maneuver here at all.

RATING: 9
Amazing game!  Plays fast and really easy to understand rules.  It also helps that all the necessary icons are on the tiles themselves.  You can clearly see how each units shoots \ fights and in which direction.

I love thematic games; theme is the 1st thing I look for in a game.  I was initially unsure about Neuroshima Hex as it looked too abstract for me; the whole puzzle element of it and the mechanics. For example, the fact that units cannot battle until a battle tile is played (or the board is full), seemed anti-thematic for me.  However, once I started playing it all fell into place.  The theme really comes through for me, and each unit \ side feels like it should.  Fast scouts, tough lumbering robots, nukes, etc.

I also enjoy the Android App version which is a great way to play NH! on the go.

RATING: 9
Fast, exciting, and very tense - it takes the strong points of games like The Duke, For the Crown, chess, etc. and mixes it with deck builder/trading card games while smoothing out the deficiencies of both.

The forced discard and small "hand" size smooths out good draws and bad draws from players and the small board size favors fast and frantic action, even if you're a defensive faction like New York.

The number and variety of other units keeps the game feeling fresh, as games will require different tactics depending on a lot of different factors.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An unbelievably complex game for something so simple. Lay a bunch of hex tiles, each of which can be attacking, defensive, carrying initiative or modifiers. Each army is different, with units which have special strengths and weaknesses.

For something so straightforward, there's a lot of depth and complexity, with various tile interactions further complicating the mix. The basic mechanisms are really straightforward - lay two of three tiles and discard the other; draw back up to three as the next person takes their turn. When a Battle tile is played, perform the actions on the tiles in order of initiative. It's genius, but it's also very easy to miss something, so there is complexity in the tile interactions which isn't always obvious and things can get missed. With so many armies, and with the variety of abilities, learning how to play them can be daunting, but the replay ability is excellent. If you're not a fan of reading swathes of icons, this probably isn't for you.

This would be a 10 if it weren't so easy to screw up the book-keeping.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The perfect two player game for me. The confined space and the clever core-mechanics grant a solid base, which the different armies can use in their unique ways to create a fast flowing, mind-blowingly exciting, deeply satisfying battle which requires fast thinking and quick adaptation. 

To make it even better, the game has the most prolific fan base, constantly pulling astonishing armies out of the hat, giving the game such variety I can't find anywhere else.

The most common complaint against NH is the randomness of the tile drawing, however it's based on mainly prejudice or after one or two plays. Knowing your armies and wisely taking risks you can effectively manage your hand to reduce the luck factor. You can still lose because of an extremely unlucky streak, true that, but most of the time the better player will win the game. Moreover, taking away the randomness would reduce the game to a sterile puzzle, as exciting as a Swiss clockwork.

RATING: 8
Totally in love with this game right now; it's my favorite in my collection.  I love the asymmetry of the armies, and the brutal immediacy of every turn.  While there is some long-term strategy, most of game is played in the HERE and NOW, where every new tile can be a game-changer.  And it's oh so satisfying when you can set up your tiles to pull off an awesome combo and you get to battle before your opponent goes and screws it all up for you.  Every game is different, and every battle feels like a mini-climax.  

A thrilling, tactical, bloody, awesome game.

Edit: (6/19/11)  I brought this down to a 9 because I don't feel the burning need to play it all the time.  I still really enjoy the game, but it's not perfect.  It's hard to put my finger on why it's not a 10, but I think it may be the AP that can occur, as well as a little too much randomness.  I still really like the game though and am looking forward to playing with my new copy of the Babel 13 expansion!

Edit: Somewhat sad to be trading this away, but it just doesn't see any plays with all the other games I have now.  Also, there's the excellent tablet version to play when I get the itch.

Edit: REACQUIRED!  Found v2.0 on the used shelf at Millennium for $20.  Took a look inside and BOTH Babel13 and Duel expansions were inside!  And, based on how the counters are bagged, I have the feeling that these were punched and never played!  I couldn't help myself.  Welcome home, friend!

RATING: 6
I'm torn on this one. It is not, strictly speaking, a wargame or pure AmeriTrash. It could easily be an abstract, and it's got a strong puzzle element.

However, the theme, board and pieces, plus the fact that there are lots of shootings, maulings and explosions, make this interesting to me.

Certainly the luck element is strong in the tile draw. However, I have not played it enough to decide whether the luck element is too strong. I'm leaning toward the conclusion that the game is short enough that I don't care.

My main complaint (again, regarding the Z-Man 2nd edition) is the horrific rulebook with tiny black print on a dark red background. Clearly this graphic design element was created by a monkey or an A.I. or someone who ate lead paint chips as a child. How in Hell did this get approved? For your sanity and your eyesight, download the BGG file with the red background stripped out and print that.

The rule book isn't especially well written, but I didn't find it to be all that bad. Certainly it's not as clear as the typical Euro rule book. But Euros only need two-page rule books (half of that being pictures of beige cubes), have no complicated moving parts and have no impenetrable mechanics other than (maybe) a scoring system that defies all sense and reason. Compared to a wargame or pure AmeriTrash classic, these rules are pretty easy.

Pay no attention to people who complain about the "math" and the simultaneous combat resolution. It is not difficult at all in practice. A couple battles should be all you need to grasp it. I'm not a math guy at all, and I have no trouble with it. Frankly, it's simpler than a lot of the "count the number of merchants in red spaces, subtract a point for every blue latrine inspector in your hand, award five points to the person who captured the Trophy of Beigeness and give five, four, three and two points, respectively, to the players with the most orange jesters alone in a space next to a foreign dignitary marker" type of scoring systems.

The components are nice, there's a reasonable amount of variety in the base game (Z-Man 2nd edition), it's a fun kind of asymmetrical and I flat out enjoy chaos and carnage. So I think it is fun for what it is and will continue to be an occasional play for me.

I'm slightly concerned that the Moloch bomb piece is too powerful. Just knowing it's out there strongly affects how you place your pieces, effectively shrinking the board.

The Android app is pretty good, too. No tile shuffling, setup or math worries.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
[microbadge=1714] [microbadge=21390]

[u]10-Sep-2016[/u]:  Neuroshima Hex! is a battle game with a very puzzly combat resolution.  It takes place in a post apocalyptic world where several different factions, which all fight in their own unique way, battle for supremacy.  It plays 2+ players, but it's best at 1v1, IMO.  But because of the random draw of unit tiles each turn and the nature of the game's initiative resolution during combat, it works quite well for me solo playing all sides.

I had played a couple hundred sessions of this game via the iOS app before finally breaking down and purchasing a physical copy.  Since then, I've played several times against myself and have a blast with it.  I do love that puzzly nature of the gameplay, and with so many different factions available, the matchups are endless.

The version I have also comes with the solitaire deck of game puzzles, but after looking them over and setting up one or two, I decided it was just too fiddly for the effort.  Only a small number of specific tiles are seeded to the board, and you are allowed to use choose from only a small handful of specific tiles to solve the problem being presented.  In that amount of time, I could almost play a complete one on one game by myself...which is preferable.

[u]18-Nov-2017[/u]:  Just a brief comment to retract my statement about the solo cards.  Yes, they are very fiddly, but after playing a few of them now, I actually enjoyed the process of setting up the specific situation, decided on my solution, and resolving the puzzle.  I would say my preferred way to play solo is still two-handed, but I am looking forward to trying more of the puzzles, especially as they increase in difficulty.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Each player chooses an army and then shuffles a stack of tiles that they draw from.  These tiles contain various troops and modules each with special powers, boosts, attacks, defense, etc.  Tiles are placed on one of the hexes (usually surrounding your base) within the small board everybody shares and they fire, defend, etc. towards certain hex sides.  Enjoy the powers and the tactical play but was nonplussed by how stagnant the board was.  Tiles are constantly added and removed but armies don't really move around.  It's sort of a multi-player tower defense game.

RATING: 8.25
Played once with four players (with no teams), three times as a two-player game, and twice as a four-player team game.  The game is far better as a two player game or a team game.

In this game players draw chips that represent units, which they then place on a board to use to attack other players' units or bases.  Each player has distinct forces that have different abilities, which require different strategies to use effectively. Battles only occur when a specific token is drawn and played by a player, and during battles _all_ units fight (not just those of one player), making timing of battles important (and also making it deliciously uncertain when a battle will occur).

The map was extremely tight with four players; it was very hard to build up combinations of characters before a fight began.  This game was fairly frantic with four players, but with two it was perfect.  There was much more strategy involved, yet the board changed frequently enough that building massive (undefendable) combos was pretty much impossible.  

This game needs at least one preliminary play through to see how it works and get a feel for the units.

[Update: Initially I gave this game a 7.5 after a four player game; it's now got 8.25, thanks to the good two-player play.]

RATING: 6
Place tiles to destroy your opponent’s tiles.

Plays: More than 100, all on iOS against the AI.
Type: VP race = a game where you race to collect victory points (fastest/most).
Complexity: Medium.
# of players: 2.
Playing time: 30 mins.
Player interaction: Conflict.
Randomness: Medium – tile draw.

Replayability: High. It’s quick so why not go again? There is also depth to explore.
Fun: The fun lies in working out the tile puzzle to your advantage. 
Memorability: Low, it’s really an abstract puzzle game.

Thematic integration: Weak. The presentation is weirdly heavy on theme, but it plays like a puzzle abstract.
Components: No comment, only played on iOS.

Who will like this: The iOS version is perfect for gamers that want a quick gaming fix when they have a minute to spare. I have a hard time seeing who would play this face to face.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
A game I really wish I could have addicted at least SOMEONE in one my of groups to- I really think this is a stellar, surprisingly difficult design. Perhaps the disconnect between expectation and reality does this one a disservice; this doesn't FEEL like one might expect a wargame to feel. There's nothing else really like it.

RATING: 9.5
New rating - 8.5 to 9.5 on 12/26/17

After many, many moons of owning this game, I finally have a moment and the wherewithal to compose a short review. This continues to be one of my favorite games of all time, in spite of (because of?) many dozens of playthroughs with veterans and novices alike. A game which FORCES tactical thinking due to a limited hand size, a constantly-changing board, and pieces with such small survivability as to be the chosen poster children for "impermanence" as an idea... keep on signing me up.

Imma keep playing.

I find Neuroshima Hex! to be endlessly intriguing. I've played it well over 100 times. It's very tactical, but it certainly pays to track your enemy's capabilities and remaining potential. I love the tension in this game: a gradual amassing of pieces, and then every so often someone lobs a tennis ball into the roomful of mousetraps and the destruction begins! RATING: 10
This game is excellent.   Plays very fast with a built in timer in that you must throw away one of your tiles every turn and when they're gone the game's over.

Truly horrible decisions to make every turn.  You must draw so that three tiles are in front of you and you MUST discard one of them never to be used again.  Most often you will find yourself and others lamenting that if you could just place all three you would have that game in the bag!  But it is not to be... Which one goes to the garbage and what will you do with the rest of them?

Strangely it's both very abstract AND very thematic at the same time.

I have come to really love this game.

I would very much like to make some custom armies for it.  With this option it has the potential to stay fresh for years and years.

RATING: 8
This is a unique and excellent design, which pits two adversaries in a tight battleground as they aim to maneuver their faction to take out the enemy's headquarters first. 

Using hexagonal units, the game plays much like an abstract as the players position their units to make the most of melee units and long range troops. As each new unit is added to the field of play, the rules of engagement can change and all manner of chain reactions can occur. 

Neuroshima Hex is as much puzzle as it is game and finding the chink in the armour of the opponent is what it is all about. 

Really great stuff.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Really fun game that is also well designed, technically and aesthetically. A few shortfalls prevent this game from being excellent. Currently I prefer the iOS version of the game.

PROS
-Quick.
-Intuitive.
-Good decisions, planning and tactical.
-Great iOS app, streamlines the game. 

CONS
-Can have a lot of account keeping during a battle, which is prone to error and increased game length. However this is eliminated in the iOS app (automatically done perfectly).
-Unfortunate luck of the draw can be devastating to your game, leaving you absolutely helpless in some cases. Furthermore winning against an opponent that was in this situation is not enjoyable at all.
-Potential uneven matches between certain decks. For instance, on the iOS version, I win consistently against all AI matches except green faction (me) versus the red faction. Red can completely nullify and out-manevoure greens special abilities and strengths easily.  

LIKE TO SEE
-One or two time chance to mulligan (re-draw) at any point during the game. Anything to minimise bad luck of the draw. 
-Have some instant actions that can be played at any point in the game (e.g. a mulligan or move troop action). These could be as cards or square chits that are not shuffled into the deck, able to use one per turn and discarded once used.

RATING: 7
Neuroshima Hex!'s iconography is far simpler to understand than I expected. To aid players, reference cards are provided for each faction.

The whole game feels like one of those Mexican standoffs that you see in action movies where two characters who don't trust each other point guns at the other's head. Then more guys pour in on either side and begin pointing guns at the other team until someone trips or coughs or something and then everyone starts firing and a bloodbath ensues. But then it happens again, and again until one side is well and truly dead.

Neuroshima Hex! is easy to learn, quick to set-up and to play, and random enough that new players have a fair chance at winning.

I only wish that I had gotten version 3.0. The graphical changes streamline the iconography a bit and the 55 solo puzzles give the game life when no one else is around to play against.

RATING: 6.3
A conceptually brilliant battle game. Quick, tactical, and confrontational - "a knife fight in a telephone booth," in the apt words of Zee Garcia. The asymmetric armies are a primary attraction but also the game's downfall, as few of the matchups end up being fair.
