RATING: 7
First play (3p) the other night. I liked it, as did one other player (the third is more of a strategy gamer and found this game a little too loose and not-his-cup-of-tea). I definitely feel that knowing your army's capabilities is huge - which means there's a learning curve to ascend. Given how much fun the first game was, it's a hill I'm looking forward to climbing. My thinking is that this might shine best as a 2p game - the three player Deathmatch was awfully tight quarters. Perhaps extending the board into the red hexes would help, as players could position their forces more strategically? I'll need to cast about the 'net to see if there are any official variants for multiplayer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
還不錯玩的戰爭版塊下棋遊戲
不過APP玩起來可能會方便很多
不然光是算誰先啟動就累了
發現現在多數電玩版桌遊都是這個領域
規則:
*(兩人規則)每人拿一個種族的35板塊洗勻蓋著，總部20滴血(圖板紀錄或是用記錄轉盤)，隨機起始，起始先放總部到圖板上，次家再放，然後手家抽一個板塊放面前可棄或保留或使用，次家抽兩個同樣使用或棄掉或保留
*遊戲目標先把對方總部20滴打完，或是結束時總部血比較多
*遊戲照順位每次面前補到三個開的板塊，棄掉一個，然後剩下的使用或棄掉或保留直到結束
*如果玩家面前三個都是使用板塊則可以全部棄掉重抽，可重複多次
*板塊分兩種，使用板塊使用完功能棄掉，單位板塊則放到圖板上
*戰鬥 當使用戰鬥板塊或是圖板放滿時啟動，照啟動數字大到小一個一個啟動板塊攻擊同數字的同回合造成傷害，超過血量的就棄掉，沒超過的放受傷標記，觸發戰鬥的玩家結算後結束回合不能棄或使用板塊
*當有玩家抽完最後一個板塊，該回合結束後對手再進行一回合後就入最後戰鬥，遊戲結束，有人抽完板塊時不能使用戰鬥板塊，平手再進行各自最後一回合在戰鬥一次(沒板塊的不能抽)，在平手就平手
**板塊簡介**
>上面數字是啟動階段，有兩個則每次戰鬥會啟動兩次
>短三角一個會往該方向相鄰格攻擊一次
>長三角遠距離往該方向攻擊第一個看到的敵軍一次
>圓弧 該方向遠距攻擊-1
>網 該方向相鄰單位放置一個網，該單位所有功能取消，不能攻擊只能被打
>箭頭 該單位每回合可以移動一格或是換方向
>盾牌 每個盾牌多一滴血
>模組 只對有長方形相連的單位有效果 可加攻擊加啟動，或是代替檔一滴血
>移動板塊 可移動一格換方向或是換方向，被網不能移動
>往後推板塊 一個友軍把敵軍往後推一格，使用後如果後方有兩個則對手選擇退到哪一格
>手榴彈 消滅一個跟你總部相鄰的單位，總部被網不能使用，不能用在總部
>狙擊 對一個單位造成一 點傷害，不能用在總部
>空襲 照上選擇移格跟相鄰格的所有單位一點傷害，包括友軍，對總部無效
*總部不能打另外一個總部
*兩個網板塊互網會互相取消沒效果，如果串聯一樣取消
*啟動數字最低是0，不會到負的
**變體***
*首家起始抽3棄1，選1使用另1只能存著，次家抽3棄1，兩塊可使用保留或棄掉
*更策略 每次補到6個，棄1，一定要保留3
**3人**
1.起始首家抽1，次家2，三家3，之後正常，任何一個玩家抽完板塊其他人在一回合就進入最後戰鬥，總部0滴的馬上淘汰板塊移除
2.設置同1但是總部起始0滴，每次造成總部傷害加一滴，先20滴的獲勝
3.1打多 單1的起始20滴，另外兩位13，順位為首次首三，起始照這個順位抽1抽2抽3之後正常，最後比總血量
**4人**
分兩隊，每人15滴血，起始照順位抽1抽2抽3，順位同隊不會連續
同隊的軍隊不互打，模組跟總部效果彼此會有作用，只是不能控制對方兵

RATING: 7
It's like Knizia's Samurai (ie playing out tiles from a limited draw from similar decks) but beefed up to allow attacks between tiles. It's clever - tiles can only hit in some directions (if they have superior initiative) and can get whacked from other directions, so there's constant turnover on the board. But it seems purely tactical and all about making the best of what you get when you get it. I liked it, but not enough to buy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A clever, well-designed game.  Each game feels different (in part because of the random-ness of tile draws), and, in general, there isn't much collateral damage (that is, even in multi-player, people really only get hit when targeted).  As the game goes on, the leader tends to quickly be targeted, keeping it pretty well balanced.  That said, detecting the endgame and being able to prepare for it is key.

Would like to play more (particularly with 3 players), but I enjoy it more and more each time with 4.  I've had one two-player play, and that's where it is definitely least likely to be naturally balanced.

*Updated* Two-player play is far more chess-like in that experience will (usually) win.  Also, this game is my kryptonite. I have won one two-player game (in a best of 3 showdown…which I lost 1 to 2), but otherwise lost every other match.

RATING: 9
Such a tight and interesting tactical game.  Build networks of threats upon threats upon counterthreats, then watch it unravel in one cataclysmic battle.  Then build it up again!  Simple yet endlessly recombinable.  Each of the factions has their own dynamic and approach which really give them a personality.  So many options in such a tiny space.  The tile draws can be brutal at times, but that is all part of the game: make the most of what you have.  Always having to discard one tile makes for delicious choices, and the simplicity of either playing, keeping or discarding the other two conceals the depth in this gem.  Wish I could play it over and over again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The components present the wrong impression of the type of game this is. NH is closer to a CCG (Magic, etc) than a hex based wargame. From the 20 hit points for the HQ's to the tile combos this is a CCG with tiles (similar in many ways to Vortex). Where it excels over the CCG's is the added complexity offered by the 'position' of the units. In Magic, a card played is effective everywhere; it simply targets the opponent. In NH, a unit must be located properly in order to deliver the maximum (or any) damage. This added complexity elevates the game beyond a typical CCG type game. 

The variety of the armies is interesting as each is sufficiently different to require unique strategies. 

Do not become attached to any particular unit as battles are frequent and devastating. It is not unusual to see the board fill and then empty several times during a match. Once players know the rules and the armies, the game last 20-30 minutes which is perfect. 

(I have heard this described as a future history version of Knizia's Samurai with combat...lots and lots of combat.)


This has been a very pleasant surprise.

*****Retrospective*****
6 - I purchased this game about a year and a half ago and it has sat unplayed on my shelf since then.  The problem with the game is it isn't impactful enough to draw repeat plays when you have to do all the bookkeeping on a battle.  Maybe I played the phone version too often, but the battle takes too long to calculate out and deal out lots of damage tokens and that's the entire point of the game.  I love the game, I love the componants, but I feel like the digital version works better because it does the combat for you.  If the hexes were bigger it might be different, but at this point I realize the only reason I haven't traded this game off is because I love the digital version too much to let go.

RATING: 7.2
A Chess game of a new age. Easy to learn, hard to master. The current amount of different armies (11) makes the game very different every time. Each army has a specific "way of winning" that you must first learn and turn out to your advantage during the game. Lots of calculation and strategy. One of the games that I can play anytime, anyday.

Avoid killing other players' armies if it's not necessary / doesn't serve your plan - you don't get points for that. Only HQ matters! This is the mistake people usually do at the beginning. 

Absolutely great iOS implementation - probably one of the best iOS games ever made.

Also, board version very nicely priced.

RATING: 7.25
I am not a big fan of sci-fi settings in general, be it in movies, books or games. There are no many sci-fi films I like and those I do like are masterpieces and there cannot be an argument regarding their quality really (Alien, 2001, Star Wars etc.). When it comes to games, the same applies. Neuroshima Hex bears a post-apocalyptic sci-fi / mad max earth setting and despite the good execution and love for detail in creating the theme kit does not do anything for me. The game however is great and I like the mixture of puzzling and drawing luck, because a game without random mechanics usually does not appeal. The different "races" or "factions" are diverse enough to make the game interesting over a long course of time, however, I found that those differences, while they are definitely in place, are not that evident and apparent during gameplay which is a shame because it would have been nice for those subtle difference to have a bigger, observable impact on the game.

RATING: 7
Great short tactical game. I play it only with 2 players, and in my opinion game is much better this way and i rate it as a 2-player version. Meaningful decisions, easy rules and great replayability are strong parts of Nueroshima Hex. Do you want to concentrate on atacking your opponent's base, defending your own or maybe whipe the board out of enemy troups? You can do whatever you want and there is a lot of possible choices and strategies brought up with many armies and simple, elegant core system. 

Iconography is unintuitive in some cases and with some combinations of armies you need to check with clear rulebooks but other than that it is a flawless 2-player abstract game for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Semi abstract with elements of engine-building and warfare. Hexagonal tiles are sequentially drawn and placed by opposing players / teams with an ultimate goal of attacking and destroying the opponent's HQ. Each tile provides unique value, such as long range damage, short range damage, protection, movement/repositioning capabilities, etc. When the board is full or a battle tile is played, warfare commences in "incentive" order, removing hit and destroyed tiles and delivering damage to each HQ. A truly unique game, I've never played anything like it.

RATING: 6
Asymmetric armies are squeezed into a confined battle plain until a battle is triggered.  Then a cascade of timing and attacks occur to inflict damage (hopefully on the opposing HQ) and clear out units.  Spiced up by the random draw of one's forces (coupled with a mandatory discard) to make this an interesting tactical game.  The random draw and limited tile options (2 per turn) means that some analysis helps, but not so much that one gets lost in an analytical fog a la chess.

RATING: 8
I have had fun with this game. Though it's abstract in a sense, the asymmetrical nature of the four factions coupled with the art give this the theme necessary for me to enjoy. As many others have described, it's analogous to a knife fight in phone booth. Normally I enjoy feeling a sentimental attachment to my cardboard troops. In this game that's the wrong feeling to have. Your troops will die quickly and die often. I do think the mechanics operate well enough with the theme and bits create a short narrative experience of gameplay. 

[b]Things I like:[/b]
:d10-1: Asymmetrical factions. Discovering those and utilizing/mitigating them as necessary gives the game a strong lure. Each faction has it's own unique feel.
:d10-2: Artwork and theme. They really help this feel like something more than an abstract.
:d10-3: The gameplay. It feels like it was playtested thoroughly. Each faction has its strengths and weaknesses. 
:d10-4: Quick play time. It's fun and possible to squeeze in more than one in a session.
:d10-5: The sudden eruption of battle when you don't expect it or don't want it. I really like how the battle system works out. It creates some tension.
:d10-6: I like the feel of hurriedly deploying my forces and reacting to the enemy's deployment.

[b]Long live the Outpost![/b]

RATING: 9
Really great game, one of my favorites. It's extremly replayable deep strategy. Easy to learn hard to master. 

Yeah the tiles drawing is random, but as your stack is drained out you can predict what you can draw. True mastery is when you track your own and your oponent stacks like this. Preparing to what might come and what cannot.

You set up traps and plans within plans. Strategy is really deep and engaging. Its like chess - at first its just straightforward simple game then you learn to employ complex strategies and you discover whole different layer of depth.

Random draft just means that good player will win most of his games but not every game. Then again its the games you loose not the ones you win that matter. If you are loosing you are learning. You stop learning when you win constantly.

My only gripe is that playing with more than 2 players is tedious and some factions become really unbalanced.

Also some add ons (Dancer and Mephisto) are like taken from another game, I dont recomend buying them at all.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
A tile-laying game in which the tiles frequently break out into battle, destroying one another in complex chain reactions.  The game takes some explaining but is fairly easy to grasp and doesn't take too long to play.  It's pretty different, so I recommend anybody who enjoys tactical combat and/or tile-laying to simply try it if possible.  This works very well as an app; in person, players will really need to focus to make sure they are evaluating the results of battle correctly, based on many initiative and combat numbers on different tiles that can also be modified by other tiles and effects.

RATING: 6
Has the optimize-what-you-drew idea of the Carcassonne family, turn-by-turn basis, which I hate. This is mitigated by knowing each other's decks and formulating a long-term plan based on what is yet to come up, but therein lies the dilemma: I think learning the decks would just create frustration that your tiles didn't come up in the order you want, or your opponent just happened to draw it at the right time. A game like Summoner Wars isn't trivial with what you drew, so it's fun to think about your turn even when you don't know the decks and what's on deck.

Reminiscent of Samurai, which has own (symmetric) decks, but has hand management.

Might grow on me.

RATING: 10
My favorite game! Always willing to play.

Pros:
-Excellent 2 player asymmetrical game
-Easy to teach/learn
-Low complexity/High depth
-Heavy tactics for short game
-Great army variety (with many expansions)
-Short game that doesn't feel like filler
-Light theme serves mechanics very well

Cons:
-Poor rule book (Improved for Ver 3.0)
-Okay with 3 players, not recommended with 4
-Luck of the draw can make for a uneven game (though this is rarer than many games with drawing mechanics)

Misc:
-Short setup/take down time
-Bags greatly help for tile drawing (face down piles are easy to knock over)

RATING: 7
A brilliant and innovative game.  The tactical depth is surprising, and every game plays out differently.  The 4 armies (I'm speaking of the base set only here) require use of different strategies.  The only negatives I have noticed:

1.  The armies are not *completely* balanced.  The Outpost, in particular, are weakest and most difficult to play effectively.  However, this can be fixed by giving them Mercenary units and possibly a couple house-rules.
2.  Bad draws (esp. in the early game) can make your life very difficult and sometimes, impossible to win a particular match.  

However - I wouldn't sacrifice the use of different (asymmetrical) armies and randomness since these elements add so much re-playability to the game.  

Update (5/20/14): I hardly play this anymore, and while I still respect the game, it doesn't hold the same appeal to me that it once did.  Downgrading to a 7.

RATING: 9
2008: 8. I've really grown to love this quick-play tactics game, but unfortunately have not been able to convince many in my gaming group to play it with me much.  Must try harder, as there's a gem of a game here.

2013 UPDATE: 9. Brought the game and its expansions to a board game day with another circle of friends and the game was a huge hit amongst all who played it!  Several had never heard of it before and two said they were going to go out and buy it right away. I still see some issues teaching non-gamers this game, but if you have friends who are already familiar with different "armies" and units in a game, then they'll get this easily.

2016: UPDATE: 10. Been playing more PvP on iPhone and I'm upgrading again. The asymmetric nature of the armies and absence of CCG/"deck building" mechanics is something I'm really enjoying. There's an element of mastery, learning the match ups and how to "pilot" a faction in different situations. And yet the armies are not too difficult to learn and games play quick. Still continue to be impressed after this game years after.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Clever tactical tile-placement game. Players draw three, discard 1 tile each turn, and places tiles on hex board. Tiles have different attack and defensive abilities. There are also tiles that grant bonuses etc. The object is to place tiles in such a way to 'hit' and deal damage to the enemy base. When all tiles have been played, the player that deals the most damage wins.

Gameplay is easy and the game doesn't get overbearing. However, it suffers from one of my gaming pet peeves...the over-reliance on iconography. All the tiles have symbols that require a reference sheet to decipher. With four different armies, that's alot of referring. Ugh. Really grinds the game down.

RATING: 7
unique little tactical battle game. Cool asymmetrical armies. I like it, but don't love it.

Both players set up the board for the next battle, and when it comes, it comes by surprise. Deterministic combat makes it pretty objective who wants to fight and who does not, but with the predictable tiles mixed with the random drawing there's a bit of drama and tension as you scramble to set up an offense, defense, a special ability tile, or a battle tile before the next battle happens.

When the board gets complicated, keeping track of who will benefit more from a battle can be pretty tedious, and it's hard to keep a running total of things because of the tactical nature of the game.

(4 Design + 3 Enjoyment)

RATING: 5.5
I'm hard pressed to call this a wargame.  This feels much more like a Knizia tile laying game.  Everything feels static, where movement, unless it comes naturally to a piece, is almost always the clear discard. The pieces, despite the art, feel abstract, in fact the whole game feels like a mathy laser puzzle.  

I feel that I might like the game more if you kept the action tiles separate where you could draw from either actions or units if you'd like this would encourage pushing for actions, or now having your actions wasted when you have no pieces on the board.  
 
All in all this game is too dependent on drawlogy for me to want to play it again.  I respect the design, but it just doesn't feel finished yet.

The game also suffers from the whole, why should I attack the leader when you are only attacking me syndrome.

Light Speed is similar enough, has just as much game, has more control, is more intense, has a sense of motion, and is done in 5 minutes. Why play this in comparison?

RATING: 7
I'm torn by this game. It's a flavorful abstract (if that makes sense). Each army has its own thematic characteristics, its plusses and minuses. Each turn is a puzzle to be solved. I like that puzzly flavor. On the downside, the game is very random. You're stuck without whatever you draw and sometimes you just draw awful. In the long run a good player will win more games than a so-so player but the short run can be very frustrating. Also, "solving" battles can be very tricky. It's quite likely you will make mistakes, or even that you and your opponent will both miss something that would have changed what happens. Purely as a game it works better on the iPad for that reason (the computer does all that battle solving). Of course, if you do both get some battle results wrong you'll never know.

RATING: 6
iPhone play only, against computer.

An abstract game, really, but with a theme that makes it feel a bit less so. It works pretty well, and I imagine it would be pretty tense in real life (esp with more 3-4 players, since the board size does NOT increase). Need to try it against a person!

4 different armies are available. These are mostly pieces (all hex tiles) that attack in 1-6 directions, adjacent and/or ranged, for 1-3 damage. They also have initiative 0-3, and possibly bonus HP (most units die after 1 hit). In addition, there is a headquarters (20 hit points; you're eliminated if it gets down to 0), modules that give some adjacent hexes bonuses or penalties, and several single-use action tiles (move tiles, attack hex adjacent to HQ, drop bomb on hex and hit it and adjacent hexes, and push a tile into an adjacent empty space). Each army has a different configuration, and will need to be played differently.

First few turns, just place your HQ. During game, draw to hand size 3, then discard 1 from game; either play, discard or keep the other two. Battles ONLY occur when you play a Battle tile, as well as a final battle at the end of the game. Battles are in descending initiative order (so low-initiative units may be killed and removed before they ever get to attack). 

The game end is triggered when a player runs out of tiles; the others get one last turn, then the final battle occurs. The player with most HP left on their HQ wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An asymmetric tactical combat-oriented tile laying game, with multiple armies vying after a very small plot of land.  Theme galore as well, drawing on the Neuroshima RPG universe.  The art is just amazing in the 3rd edition.

While it is not uniquely better than any of the pure games of a certain genre (eg, it does not beat games like El Grande for area control) the fact that it meshes all those element together so seamlessly is what makes this special.  While there are some complaints about it being "fiddly" the rules are nowhere near as complex as a wargame, and can be easily explained.

This game also ranks high for me, because the theme comes through in a very strong way.  Also, what you are doing makes sense game wise.  You can develop a narrative. You feel like you are there, in the trenches of this post apocalyptic wasteland

RATING: 9
An excellent tactical battle game that plays quickly and delivers incredible bang for the buck. I particularly like asymmetric games, and Neuroshima Hex shines in that regard. The units don't just differ slightly; the four armies provided each have a unique feel and require different approaches to be successful.

Combat is resolved deterministically, but the order in which tiles are drawn introduces a random element that players must contend with. Some draws will be no-brainers in terms of what to discard and what to play, but I've yet to see a full game that didn't provide numerous tough decisions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
It's probably not really fair to rate this game after a single 2-player game, since it seems like the type of game whose rating would go up with more plays and an increased familiarity with the various components, but I'm not always a fair person!  So anyhow, THIS is my type of tile-laying game!!  You've got melee attacks, ranged attacks, grenade attacks, sniper attacks... all sorts of attacks, really!  Mind you, as a result of all that attacking, the tiles that come into play don't often remain in play for very long, which might take some getting used to for those of us who are accustomed to other tile-laying games.  But I absolutely love that each army is a completely unique play experience with various strengths and weaknesses.  And there are no farmers in this game - the players only have to worry about the likes of Butchers, Hunter-Killers, Annihilators, and Gladiators, none of whom are as sneaky as farmers!  It's a cool game, but I do have some gripes about the physical components.  First of all, the theme doesn't grab me at all and I find the artwork to be rather cheesy, but that's purely personal preference.  Secondly, when you buy this game, you are paying for a lot of Chinese air - the game box should have been about 1/4 of it's current size.  Thirdly, I find the rulebook to be nearly unreadable (even with my reading glasses on!) due to small font on a dark background.  And finally, if they're going to have such a big box, they should have at least made the rest of the components larger so that players can tell at a glance what the board situation is without having to hover over it too closely.  But that griping aside, it's a fun, light, quick-playing game that I'm looking forward to trying with 3 and 4 players to see how that changes the play experience.  Game design: 3/5  Personal enjoyment: 3.5/5  Total: 6/10

RATING: 5.8

Update: I've now played it once as a 5 player game and twice as a 2 player game.

The player elimination kinda sucks, but it was much less complicated to calculate initiative etc than I thought it would be.

I've played this ALOT on the iPhone and am increasingly of the opinion that the starting tiles can decide the game. Get bad tiles and you can be 10 hit points down by turn 2. Get good tiles and you can turtle your base or set the whole game up too easily.

Rating down from a 7 to a 5.8.

RATING: 9
For 3 players this game is bad (atack the leader), for 4 the board is too small, team variant is ok, but there`re much better games for 4 players. But for 2 players Neuroshima Hex is great.

Very often you want use all three tiles and it`s not always easy to choose wchich one do you discard. Interesting is: i hit him for 3, he hit me this unit, but in phase 2, so i hit him first in phase 3 and so on. Maybe during the first it seems to complicated for such fast and easy game, but after few plays it`s not so hard.

The game is very tactical, but you will play better when you know the armys. Nice that every army is diferent. But you will see the character of outpost only in the 2 player game.

Sometimes you draw only bad tiles and you can only loose, but usually the game is very interesting. After the battle the boards usually clears and you start from the beginning. So the game is very dynamic.

The King of the hill (second side of the first edition board): not so good. You must put tiles on the hill, also when you don`t need it - your oponnent wouldn`t have a adventage. So you don`t have so much choices like in the standard game. With Borgo this variant is broken! Borgo has a very high initiative and with the bonuses from the hill they are unbeatable.

RATING: 8.4
This game pushes all the right buttons for me. I'm not even sure what to compare it to, but the whole strategically setting up your pieces for a potential battle where all attacks trigger at once, not sure why but that makes for a really fun abstract. I, like many others, feel Neuroshima Hex! lends itself to app play, as there is almost like this extra player, ie the 'computer', which resolves the battles and reminds you how much of an idiot you are when you forget to see that 4 on your opponent's sniper at the back of his base. And yeah, it can get complex. You really need to pay attention to the interactions of all the pieces, like any good abstract. 

I'm not a big fan of more than two players, as it just devolves into this chaotic mess, but as a two player strategy game? Wow.

RATING: 8.5
Might just very well slip into the top 10 of my favorite games.  I cannot seem to win, but I think that the game is tremendous.  Basically an abstract, which is shocking to me, because I have very little interest in abstracts (see: Attika), it can be decided at times by the draw of the tiles.  The game is designed so that you feel so pinched at nearly all times--there is just no way to really bust out and lay waste to the enemy most of the time.  

The theme is very interesting (post-apocalyptic warfare), the bits are very well done (though many of my tiles have slipped printing), and the artwork is incredible.  It is one of the most interesting looking games that I have seen.  Outside of those, the gameplay is smooth, quick, fighting is frequent and bloody, and the replayability is immense.  

Highly recommended after a half dozen plays.

RATING: 10
Fantastic fast-paced 2-player game. Cool tactical decisions. You can play it really off-the-cuff or take your time figuring out optimal positions for combat and still have a good experience. The battle system is cool, although with a busy battlefield it sometimes takes a little while to fully run through the battle in your head. With a little practice, this gets easier. Also, as tactical as the game is, there's still room for wider strategy.

They did a great job at giving the armies a different flavor. Borgo is always right up in your face, often with a lot of suicide attacks at the opponent's HQ (unless a Net Fighter is safely tucked at the HQ as well, then it's beatin' time). Moloch fights from afar, building a bunker and pushing people away for a safe buffer. Outpost is tough to predict due to its high mobility, and Hegemony dabbles in several things. Things like this make it very replayable.

The expansion armies are very neat too, but what brings this to an even further level is the Duel board. Highly recommended after a couple plays.

RATING: 7
I finally got to try this in a 3-player frenzy.  Nice game left me feeling like I wanted to play lots more.  The crux is placement of hex-army tokens on a small hex map in particular orientation, defending each player's headquarters.  Armies are different for each player, and drawn randomly.  After the board fills up or a player chooses to play a battle token, all hell breaks loose in a predetermined order based on the turn order hierarchy on each army tile.  There is a complexity of combat effects. Really fun and interesting. I also have the iPhone version which plays great!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Purchased from Mind Games, Melbourne, Australia.

I love this game. It's got a certain quality that makes it instantly recognizable as a great game. I have played games that grow on you, and I get that, but it's nice to have games like this, that grab you from the moment you play your first tile. 

It has been compared to Hive. I can see why, but I feel that the comparison can be misleading, as this game somehow manages to capture the feel of a combat game. It has some (perhaps a lot) of the feel of a tactical miniatures game. It doesn't hurt that the units capture the imagination. 

Fantastic game!

RATING: 9
Pros:
+Strong and unique mechanics
+Quick asymmetric game
+Love the initiative system
+Lots of factions available through expansions, deep options out there

Cons:
-Some will be bothered by the 'calculations' (not mathematical) in each battle to determine who attacks who and when.  I like this, some don't. 
-Luck factor is a bit high in the tiles you choose.  The better play will still win more on average, and the games are relatively short, but bad draws can really cripple you at the wrong time. 

I really enjoy Neuroshima Hex!  It's a great 2-player option, unique from most other games, plays quick but deep, and I love asymmetric games with many factions available.  Really good and fun game that will get a lot of mileage, especially with inexpensive expansions.

RATING: N/A
Neuroshima Hex is a strategy game placed in a post-apocalyptic world of Neuroshima (a Polish RPG). Each player leads one of four armies: Borgo, Hegemonia (Hegemony), Moloch and Posterunek (Outpost). Each army deck consists of 34 tiles - soldiers, support tiles and special actions. You win when you destroy all enemy H.Q. or your H.Q. is the least damaged one at the end. A second edition of the game was released at Essen 2007 with updated graphics and a new, larger board. A special Essen pack sold there also included the Neuroshima Hex! - Doomsday Machine fifth army expansion. Expanded By: Neuroshima Hex! - Mad Bomber, Neuroshima Hex! - Doomsday Machine

RATING: N/A
Neuroshima Hex is a strategy game placed in a post-apocalyptic world of Neuroshima  (a Polish RPG).
Each player leads one of four armies: Borgo, Hegemonia (Hegemony), Moloch and Posterunek (Outpost). Each army deck consists of 34 tiles - soldiers, support tiles and special actions. You win when you destroy all enemy H.Q. or your H.Q. is the least damaged one at the end.
\13\\13\
Note: This may be combined with Neuroshima Hex! Duel to give you different armies and to play with 5-6 players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The strategic learning curve on this is pretty steep, since each army plays in a completely unique manner. The iconography isn't all that clear either, at least for someone who doesn't play on fairly regular basis. Those are my only (not to mention very minor) complaints, it does a nice job of putting an interesting wargame skin on what is really just an abstract tile laying game. Neuroshima Hex! is honestly one of the best games of this type I have played. It reminds me of Hive, but with much more chrome and complexity (in a good way). The ios/android app is also very good, but do yourself a favor and get a hard copy to learn on. The player aids in the box are well worth it when you are learning the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
One of the most fruitful creative areas in game design lately seems to be taking two disjoint genres and merging them into a quirky and awesome game.  Video game designers, especially, have been exploring this concept (see: Puzzle Quest and Henry Hatsworth).  This is the idea behind Neuroshima Hex.  It's half tile-laying game, half fighty game.  Both halves are simple by their respective genres' standards, but together they are so much greater than the sum of these parts.

The coolest aspect is that each army is unique and plays completely differently--I love asymmetry in my games, especially confrontational ones.  The whole module/unit/action tile division allows a lot of flexibility in army design.  The subsequent expansions and fan armies offer wildly different experiences.

Just a fantastic game.  One of my all-time favorites.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Super fantastic game.  The strategy behind this game is phenomenal.  I love the build up to the gunfight.  I feel like you can feel the tension growing as the board slowly starts filling up before the battle. I love that this game is pretty easy to pick up and play but the level of strategy is intense.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
My "breakthrough game" - Neuroshima Hex! got me into modern boardgaming and if it wasn't for this game I probably wouldn't be into this hobby at all.  

It used to be my #1 top rated game for years, but right now it was slightly overshadowed by a few other games in my collection (I'm probably a bit burnt out on it after so many plays) . I still love playing it though, it's a brilliant, timeless design and it will foverer hold the honorable spot on my shelf. Sure, there's luck in the tile draw, but it doesn't really matter in the long run - the better player will win most of the time. The amount of fun and replayability packed into this one box is simply astonishing. 

[b]/update, February 2018:[/b] "Objectivelly speaking" it's a 10, I've downgraded it to a 9 just because I'm a little burnt out on it after countless app plays.

[i]I own the following expansions: Mississippi, New York, Sharrash, Uranopolis, Vegas, Neojungle, Smart, Steel Police, Mephisto, Dancer, Death Breath, Iron Gang.[/i]

RATING: 7
This is totally a cheat. I’ve played this before, but this is the first time I’ve ever played against someone. Also, we played using the iOS version of this game, mostly because my opponent lives all the way across the continent. Still, I think it qualifies as “not played before” and I log all of my online and iOS games when I’m playing against someone I know.

Now, about the game. It’s a thematic abstract game where you place tiles and try to destroy your opponent’s base. The tiles you place (I’ll call them army tiles since they look like soldiers) typically have damage on them that either damages tiles that are right next to it or in the same row depending on the attack type. The army tiles sometimes have other abilities like shields (to prevent ranged damage), nets (to prevent other tiles from attacking), extra life, the ability to move later, or other abilities (depending on the army). There are also tiles that let you move your tiles, your opponents tiles, attack a specific unit, a few few units, and start battles.

Battles are where everything goes down. Each of those army tiles has an initiative between 3 and 0. Starting at initiative 3 and all the way down to 0 (usually just the bases), every tile will attack. Any tiles destroyed are removed from the game. Those battles are started either with a battle tile or when the board is completely full.

In addition, there are multiple armies that all work roughly the same but have different wrinkles. One army might be more mobile, or have more armor, or use more nets than the other.

So we have a thematic game that functions a lot like an abstract game. There are lots of interesting mechanics and asymmetric factions. It can play anywhere from two to four, but most call it a two player game. There are additional factions available for purchase and the game is on it’s 3rd (or 4th) incarnation. You can play it cheaply on iOS or Android. That’s a lot of strengths.

Yet, I’m a little lukewarm on all of the factions. Some of them are just downright confusing and while the interface has a way to look up the different powers of each tile, it’s not as slick as other games. That being said, you can ignore most of the factions and happily stick to the four the game comes with, buying the additional ones later. That would be my recommendation.

This game is at least worth a purchase if you own an iPad, iPhone, or Android device. It’s a quick game that has some pretty interesting decisions to be made. I’m considering buying the physical edition because I like this one so much.

RATING: N/A
Post-apocalyptic sci-fi, hex tile placement, strategy game on a city ruin board map. Somewhat abstract puzzle with interactive battling. Has unique game play elements to me. No cards. Strategically place and face your units and time placement of special effect tiles.  Factions have different characteristics that play differently. Best part of the game is the bluff and counter and how it encourages tenuous negotiations between players.  Has a generous amount of iconology to get through.

RATING: 9
Wow, I didn't expect that. I think it's one of the very few Polish games that are actually popular outside of Poland. And man, am I not surprised: this game, being quite easy to learn, can be very complicated. This is pure tactics, and the theme is damn awesome. Different factions have different approaches to the game, and I find mechanics very much connected to their backgrounds. There's not that much luck involved, so if you're more strategy-oriented, don't even think - just buy this awesome game.
Well, the components could be something better than simple hexagonal cardboards, but they're good enough for me.

RATING: 7
I'm pretty sure we missed a lot of the rules in our first attempt at Neuroshima Hex since we were pressed for time.  Our explainer gave us a very brief overview of the mechanics but did not went into details of what each symbol represented, the order of conflict resolution, as well as how units affected one another.  So my initial impression was a game very reminiscent of MTG, albeit simpler and less cleanly designed (tiles just don't look as good as cards, nor carry as much information).  It also felt somewhat chaotic, random, and especially unbalanced for 3 players, since the third player can just hold back and watch the other two destroy each other.

I think Neuroshima Hex has potential and I would definitely want to try it again with a full understanding of the gameplay elements.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.6
I feel very mixed up about this one. There’s a lot going on, while you’re playing. There’s a pretty important amount of luck involved in this game, because you have to draw tiles blindly. In one turn, you could draw three almost, at that moment, useless tiles. While in the next turn, you draw three tiles which you want to play all. The theme of the game isn’t that great to me. Personally I prefer a theme which shows some more history (WWII, Spanish War, Roman war), but that doesn’t change the gameplay. The gameplay is different from other wargames I’ve played before. No dice included for fighting. To destroy an enemies unit, you just have to do one thing. Hit it (or maybe hit it two times, if the unit has gain an extra protection). That’s it. And still it is very tactical. Every unit you place, you should think about how it would be rotated onto the board, because that could make a huge difference. Played this game with 4 players, but I think it might work better with just two players.

While I was playing this one, I was feeling like I was in the middle of a computer game instead of a boardgame. Could work nice on a tablet.

RATING: 7
This is a great combat game. The asymmetrical teams add replay value, and the random unit draws force players to adapt their tactics.

With 4 players, the metagame can really shine through as well. The board gets so tight that players need to make deals (at least temporarily) to get what they really need.

This is not a game that I would play if I wanted a strategic matchup, however. There is too much randomness to have any major control.

RATING: 10
Highly tactical game that looks abstract but feels like a post-apocalyptic brawl over a spit of land. The different factions are cool and allow variety, though you're likely to play the same one many times in order to get to know them/identify with them (can't say that about an abstract game).

The expansions, Babel13 and Duel, add plenty of fun in the armies alone, while also providing variations to the basic system that can be tried out to mess around a bit.

Own all the expansions.
